<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Aegilops tauschii draft genome sequence reveals a gene repertoire for wheat adaptation"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2013 496:7443"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-03-24"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sequencing and analysing the diploid genome and transcriptome of Aegilops tauschii provide new insights into the role of this genome in enabling the adaptation of bread wheat and are a step towards understanding the very large and complicated hexaploid genomes of wheat species. The hexaploid genome of bread wheat Triticum aestivum, designated AABBDD, evolved as a result of hybridization between three ancestral grasses. Two papers published in the issue of Nature present genome sequences and analysis of two of these wheat progenitors. First, the genome sequence of the diploid wild wheat T. urartu (ancestor of the A genome), which resembles cultivated wheat more strongly than either Aegilops speltoides (the B ancestor) or Ae. tauschii (the D donor). And second, the Ae. tauschii genome, together with an analysis of its transcriptome. These genomes and their analyses will be powerful tools for the study of complex, polyploid wheat genomes and a valuable resource for genetic improvement of wheat. About 8,000 years ago in the Fertile Crescent, a spontaneous hybridization of the wild diploid grass Aegilops tauschii (2n = 14; DD) with the cultivated tetraploid wheat Triticum turgidum (2n = 4x = 28; AABB) resulted in hexaploid wheat (T. aestivum; 2n = 6x = 42; AABBDD)1,2. Wheat has since become a primary staple crop worldwide as a result of its enhanced adaptability to a wide range of climates and improved grain quality for the production of baker&#8217;s flour2. Here we describe sequencing the Ae. tauschii genome and obtaining a roughly 90-fold depth of short reads from libraries with various insert sizes, to gain a better understanding of this genetically complex plant. The assembled scaffolds represented 83.4% of the genome, of which 65.9% comprised transposable elements. We generated comprehensive RNA-Seq data and used it to identify 43,150 protein-coding genes, of which 30,697 (71.1%) were uniquely anchored to chromosomes with an integrated high-density genetic map. Whole-genome analysis revealed gene family expansion in Ae.&#8201;tauschii of agronomically relevant gene families that were associated with disease resistance, abiotic stress tolerance and grain quality. This draft genome sequence provides insight into the environmental adaptation of bread wheat and can aid in defining the large and complicated genomes of wheat species."/>

           " /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="King Abdulaziz University, Jeddah 21589, Saudi Arabia" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.412125.1, 0000 0001 0619 1117, King Abdulaziz University, Jeddah 21589, Saudi Arabia, &#xA;                  " /></span></sup>Â </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs3" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs3">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs3-content"><p>About 8,000 years ago in the Fertile Crescent, a spontaneous hybridization of the wild diploid grass <i>Aegilops tauschii</i> (2<i>n</i> = 14; DD) with the cultivated tetraploid wheat <i>Triticum turgidum</i> (2<i>n</i> = 4<i>x</i> = 28; AABB) resulted in hexaploid wheat (<i>T. aestivum</i>; 2<i>n</i> = 6<i>x</i> = 42; AABBDD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Salamini, F., Ozkan, H., Brandolini, A., Schafer-Pregl, R. &amp; Martin, W. Genetics and geography of wild cereal domestication in the near east. Nature Rev. Genet. 3, 429â441 (2002)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1027">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Dubcovsky, J. &amp; Dvorak, J. Genome plasticity a key factor in the success of polyploid wheat under domestication. Science 316, 1862â1866 (2007)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1030">2</a></sup>. Wheat has since become a primary staple crop worldwide as a result of its enhanced adaptability to a wide range of climates and improved grain quality for the production of bakerâs flour<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Dubcovsky, J. &amp; Dvorak, J. Genome plasticity a key factor in the success of polyploid wheat under domestication. Science 316, 1862â1866 (2007)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1034">2</a></sup>. Here we describe sequencing the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome and obtaining a roughly 90-fold depth of short reads from libraries with various insert sizes, to gain a better understanding of this genetically complex plant. The assembled scaffolds represented 83.4% of the genome, of which 65.9% comprised transposable elements. We generated comprehensive RNA-Seq data and used it to identify 43,150 protein-coding genes, of which 30,697 (71.1%) were uniquely anchored to chromosomes with an integrated high-density genetic map. Whole-genome analysis revealed gene family expansion in <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> of agronomically relevant gene families that were associated with disease resistance, abiotic stress tolerance and grain quality. This draft genome sequence provides insight into the environmental adaptation of bread wheat and can aid in defining the large and complicated genomes of wheat species.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>We selected <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> accession AL8/78 for genome sequencing because it has been extensively characterized genetically (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). Using a whole genome shotgun strategy, we generated 398âGb of high-quality reads from 45 libraries with insert sizes ranging from 200âbp to 20âkb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). A hierarchical, iterative assembly of short reads employing the parallelized sequence assembler SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1095">3</a></sup> achieved contigs with an N50 length (minimum length of contigs representing 50% of the assembly) of 4,512âbp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Paired-end information combined with an additional 18.4âGb of Roche/454 long-read sequences was used sequentially to generate 4.23-Gb scaffolds (83.4% were non-gapped contiguous sequences) with an N50 length of 57.6âkb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). The assembly represented 97% of the 4.36-Gb genome as estimated by <i>K</i>-mer analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). We also obtained 13,185 <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> expressed sequence tag (EST) sequences using Sanger sequencing, of which 11,998 (91%) could be mapped to the scaffolds with more than 90% coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Overall statistics of sequencing and genome assembly</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature12028/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To aid in gene identification, we performed RNA-Seq (53.2âGb for a 117-Mb transcriptome assembly) on 23 libraries representing eight tissues including pistil, root, seed, spike, stamen, stem, leaf and sheath (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). Using both evidence-based and <i>de novo</i> gene predictions, we identified 34,498 high-confidence protein-coding loci. FGENESH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516â522 (2000)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1398">4</a></sup> and GeneID models were supported by a 60% overlap with either our ESTs and RNA-Seq reads, or with homologous proteins. More than 76% of the gene models had a significant match (Eâvalueââ¤â10<sup>â5</sup>; alignment lengthââ¥â60%) in the GenBank non-redundant database. An additional 8,652 loci were predicted as low-confidence genes as a result of incomplete gene structure or limited expression data support (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). We also predicted a total of 2,505 transfer RNA, 358 ribosomal RNA, 35 small nuclear RNA and 78 small nucleolar RNA genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>).</p><p>We found that more than 65.9% of the <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genome was composed of different transposable element (TE) families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). About 5âÃâ10<sup>6</sup> Illumina reads of <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> were mapped to hexaploid wheat repetitive sequences and we found that a comparable percentage of reads (more than 62.3%) could be classified as part of a TE sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). This estimate is similar to that derived from a previous survey of Roche/454 sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wicker, T. et al. A whole-genome snapshot of 454 sequences exposes the composition of the barley genome and provides evidence for parallel evolution of genome size in wheat and barley. Plant J. 59, 712â722 (2009)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1429">5</a></sup>. There were 410 different TE families, of which the 20 most abundant contributed more than 50% of the <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 9</a>). A single peak of increased insertion activity was estimated to occur about 3â4âMyr ago by measuring the similarity of the assembled LTR retrotransposons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>), suggesting that the expansion of the <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genome was relatively recent and coincided with the abrupt climate change during the Pliocene Epoch<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Williams, M. et al. Pliocene climate and seasonality in North Atlantic shelf seas. Phil. Trans. R. Soc. A 367, 85â108 (2009)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1445">6</a></sup>.</p><p>We constructed a high-density genetic map using an F<sub>2</sub> population of 490 individuals derived from a cross between the <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> accessions Y2280 and AL8/78. The map, whose total length was 1059.8âcentimorgans (cM), consisted of 151,083 single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers developed by restriction-site-associated DNA (RAD) tag sequencing technology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Together with bin-mapped wheat ESTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Qi, L. L. et al. A chromosome bin map of 16,000 expressed sequence tag loci and distribution of genes among the three genomes of polyploid wheat. Genetics 168, 701â712 (2004)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1460">7</a></sup>, SNPs and tags<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Poland, J. A., Brown, P. J., Sorrells, M. E. &amp; Jannink, J. L. Development of high-density genetic maps for barley and wheat using a novel two-enzyme genotyping-by-sequencing approach. PLoS ONE 7, e32253 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1464">8</a></sup>, the genetic map was used to align 30,303 scaffolds (1.72âGb; 30,697 genes) to chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). The <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genes and scaffolds were also anchored to barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mayer, K. F. et al. Unlocking the barley genome by chromosomal and comparative genomics. Plant Cell 23, 1249â1263 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1475">9</a></sup> and <i>Brachypodium</i> chromosome maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative. Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763â768 (2010)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1482">10</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). Calculation of <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ratios (the ratio of non-synonymous substitutions to synonymous substitutions) for pairs of conserved orthologous genes showed that the average values between <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> and barley (20,892 genes), <i>Brachypodium</i> (17,231 genes), rice (16,370 genes) and sorghum (18,623 genes) were 0.2214, 0.1888, 0.1736 and 0.1726, respectively, which indicated that most gene lineages evolved under purifying selection in <i>Ae.âtauschii</i>. A total of 628 genes exhibited <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ratios of more than 0.8 when compared with the other four species, indicating potential positive selection (innermost circle of <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). These genes were assigned to a wide range of molecular functions by using Gene Ontology (GO) analyses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 14</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Comparative analysis of  Ae.âtauschii  ordered scaffolds versus barley and  Brachypodium."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: <b>Comparative analysis of </b> <b><i>Ae.âtauschii</i></b> <b> ordered scaffolds versus barley and </b> <b><i>Brachypodium</i></b>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12028/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12028/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12028_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12028/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12028_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="709" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The inner circle represents the seven <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> chromosomes scaled according to the genetic map incorporating genome scaffolds. Red points show the <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> ratios between anchored <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genes and their putative orthologues in <i>Brachypodium.</i> Moving outwards, the second circle compares <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> against the seven barley chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Mayer, K. F. et al. Unlocking the barley genome by chromosomal and comparative genomics. Plant Cell 23, 1249â1263 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1571">9</a></sup>. The heatmaps show the density distribution of barley cDNA loci that are aligned with <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genes. The outer two circles illustrate <i>Brachypodium</i> chromosomes according to conserved synteny with <i>Ae.âtauschii</i>. The coloured lines below each chromosome identify putative orthologous gene pairs between <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genes, barley genes and <i>Brachypodium</i> genes.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM73">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12028/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>Ae.âtauschii</i> proteins were clustered with those of <i>Brachypodium</i>, rice, sorghum and barley (full-length complementary DNAs), and formed 23,202 orthologous groups (at least two members; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). In total, we identified 11,289 (barley/<i>Ae.âtauschii</i>) and 14,675 (<i>Brachypodium</i>/<i>Ae.âtauschii</i>) orthologous gene pairs. We found that 8,443 gene groups contained sequences from all five grass genomes, and 234 were specific to Pooideae (<i>Ae.âtauschii</i>, <i>Brachypodium</i> and barley) and 587 were specific to Triticeae (<i>Ae.âtauschii</i> and barley) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). Enrichment analyses of both Pfam domains and GO terms showed that genes encoding NBS-LRR (nucleotide-binding-site leucine-rich repeat) proteins were over-represented in <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> relative to <i>Brachypodium</i> and rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="McHale, L., Tan, X., Koehl, P. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Plant NBS-LRR proteins: adaptable guards. Genome Biol. 7, 212 (2006)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1646">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Luo, S. et al. Dynamic nucleotide-binding-site and leucine-rich-repeat-encoding genes in the grass family. Plant Physiol. 159, 197â210 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1649">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). These observations are consistent with those reported in a recent study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Brenchley, R. et al. Analysis of the bread wheat genome using whole-genome shotgun sequencing. Nature 491, 705â710 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1656">13</a></sup>. A total of 1,219 <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genes were similar to NBS-LRR genes (R gene analogues (RGAs))<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="McHale, L., Tan, X., Koehl, P. &amp; Michelmore, R. W. Plant NBS-LRR proteins: adaptable guards. Genome Biol. 7, 212 (2006)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1663">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Yue, J. X., Meyers, B. C., Chen, J. Q., Tian, D. &amp; Yang, S. Tracing the origin and evolutionary history of plant nucleotide-binding site-leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR) genes. New Phytol. 193, 1049â1063 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1666">14</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). This number is double that in rice (623) and sixfold that in maize (216)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Luo, S. et al. Dynamic nucleotide-binding-site and leucine-rich-repeat-encoding genes in the grass family. Plant Physiol. 159, 197â210 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1674">12</a></sup>, indicating that the RGA family has substantially expanded in <i>Ae.âtauschii</i>. We mapped 878 RGAs (72%) to specific positions across wheat chromosomes by using molecular markerâgenome sequence alignment, which provides a large number of potential disease resistance loci for further investigation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="&#xA;                        Ae.âtauschii&#xA;                         gene families and transcription factors.&#xA;                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: 
                        <b><i>Ae.âtauschii</i></b>
                        <b> gene families and transcription factors.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12028/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12028/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12028_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12028/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12028_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="283" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of orthologous gene families in <i>Ae.âtauschii</i>, <i>Brachypodium</i>, sorghum, rice and barley. The number of gene families is represented in each intersection of the Venn diagram. The first number under the species name indicates the total number of genes annotated for a particular species, and the second indicates the number of genes in groups for that organism. The difference between the two accounts for singleton genes that were not present in any cluster. <b>b</b>, The composition of transcription factors (TFs) in <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> and <i>Brachypodium</i> composed of more than 30 members.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM74">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12028/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We found more genes for the cytochromeâP450 family in <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> (485) than in sorghum (365), rice (333), <i>Brachypodium</i> (262) or maize (261). This family of genes is important for abiotic stress response, especially in biosynthetic and detoxification pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schuler, M. A. &amp; Werck-Reichhart, D. Functional genomics of P450s. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 54, 629â667 (2003)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1740">15</a></sup>. Using 178 manually curated cold-acclimation-related genes such as the CCAAT-binding factor (CBF) transcription factors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Thomashow, M. F. Molecular basis of plant cold acclimation: insights gained from studying the CBF cold response pathway. Plant Physiol. 154, 571â577 (2010)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1744">16</a></sup>, late-embryogenesis-abundant proteins (LEA) and osmoprotectant biosynthesis proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>) as queries, we identified 216 cold-related genes in the <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genome, in contrast to 164 genes in <i>Brachypodium</i>, 132 in rice, 159 in sorghum and 148 in maize. Some of these genes were specific to <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> or to Pooideae, including those encoding ice-recrystallization inhibition proteinâ1 precursor, DREB2 transcription factorâÎ± isoform and cold-responsive LEA/RAB-related COR protein. Expression analysis of RNA-Seq data showed that most of these <i>Ae.âtauschii</i>-specific and Pooideae-specific genes were constitutively expressed in <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 23</a>). In addition, 1,489 transcription factors (TFs) in 56 families were identified by using Pfam DNA-binding domains (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> had an excess of such TFs as MYB-related genes (103, in contrast with 66 in <i>Brachypodium</i> and 95 in maize), and these are also thought to be involved in various stress responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Lata, C., Yadav, A. &amp; Prasad, M. in Abiotic Stress Response in PlantsâPhysiological, Biochemical and Genetic Perspectives ( Shanker, A. &amp; Venkateswarlu, B., eds) 269â296 (InTech, 2011)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1780">17</a></sup>. The M-type MADS-box genes (58, in contrast with 23 in <i>Brachypodium</i> and 34 in maize) are involved in regulation in plant reproduction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Masiero, S., Colombo, L., Grini, P. E., Schnittger, A. &amp; Kater, M. M. The emerging importance of type I MADS box transcription factors for plant reproduction. Plant Cell 23, 865â872 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1787">18</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 18</a>). ARACNe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Margolin, A. et al. ARACNE: an algorithm for the reconstruction of gene regulatory networks in a mammalian cellular context. BMC Bioinformatics 7 (Suppl. 1). S7 (2006)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1798">19</a></sup> co-expression analysis using RNA-Seq data predicted an expression network of 1,283 interactions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 25</a>), in which 13 TFs were associated with the expression of drought tolerance genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Mao, X. et al. Transgenic expression of TaMYB2A confers enhanced tolerance to multiple abiotic stresses in Arabidopsis. Funct. Integr. Genomics 11, 445â465 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1805">20</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 20</a>).</p><p>We predicted a total of 159 (133 families) previously undescribed microRNAs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>), and identified segmental and tandem duplications in 42 members of the miR2118 family that were organized into two groups on 15 scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 26</a>). The miR399 family, which is involved in the regulation of inorganic phosphate homeostasis in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Hu, B. &amp; Chu, C. Phosphate starvation signaling in rice. Plant Signal. Behav. 6, 927â929 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1821">21</a></sup>, was expanded (20 members in <i>Ae.âtauschii</i>, compared with 11 in rice and 10 in maize), and may contribute to the ability of <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> to grow in low-nutrient soils. The expansion of the miR2275 family (eight members in <i>Ae.âtauschii</i>, compared with two in rice and four in maize) may contribute to the enhanced disease resistance of <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> because phased short interfering RNAs initiated by miR2275 have been implicated in these activities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Shivaprasad, P. V. et al. A microRNA superfamily regulates nucleotide binding site-leucine-rich repeats and other mRNAs. Plant Cell 24, 859â874 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1838">22</a></sup>.</p><p>The <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genome served as the source for many grain quality genes in hexaploid wheat, creating a step improvement in the formation of the elastic dough essential for bread making<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Dubcovsky, J. &amp; Dvorak, J. Genome plasticity a key factor in the success of polyploid wheat under domestication. Science 316, 1862â1866 (2007)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1848">2</a></sup>. Grain quality genes include high-molecular-weight glutenin subunits (HMW-GS), low-molecular-weight glutenin subunits (LMW-GS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Gupta, R. B., Singh, N. K. &amp; Shepherd, K. W. The cumulative effect of allelic variation in LMW and HMW glutenin subunits on dough properties in the progeny of two bread wheats. Theor. Appl. Genet. 77, 57â64 (1989)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1852">23</a></sup>, grain texture proteins (GSP; puroindolines)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Chantret, N. et al. Molecular basis of evolutionary events that shaped the hardness locus in diploid and polyploid wheat species (Triticum and Aegilops). Plant Cell 17, 1033â1045 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1856">24</a></sup> and storage protein activator (SPA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ravel, C. et al. Nucleotide polymorphism in the wheat transcriptional activator Spa influences its pattern of expression and has pleiotropic effects on grain protein composition, dough viscoelasticity, and grain hardness. Plant Physiol. 151, 2133â2144 (2009)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1860">25</a></sup>. We identified two <i>HMW-GS</i> genes, five <i>LMW-GS</i> genes, one <i>Pina</i> gene, two <i>Pinb</i> genes, one <i>GSP</i> gene and one <i>SPA</i> gene in the <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genome sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). As has been shown for the <i>Hardness</i> (<i>Ha</i>) locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Chantret, N. et al. Molecular basis of evolutionary events that shaped the hardness locus in diploid and polyploid wheat species (Triticum and Aegilops). Plant Cell 17, 1033â1045 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d56593e1896">24</a></sup>, the <i>GSP</i>, <i>Pina</i> and <i>Pinb</i> genes were also organized in a cluster. RNA-Seq analysis showed that these grain quality genes were expressed predominantly in seeds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 29</a>).</p><p>The anchoring of more than 40% of the scaffold sequences to four genetic maps and to syntenic regions of other sequenced grass species provided a structural framework for integrating multiple maps by using shared markers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). The co-localization of genes in scaffolds and genetically mapped quantitative trait loci (QTLs) will directly support map-based gene cloning. On chromosome 2D, for example, the locations of 33 QTLs or genes were integrated with scaffold information (<a href="http://ccg.murdoch.edu.au/cmap/ccg-live/">http://ccg.murdoch.edu.au/cmap/ccg-live/</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). Alignment of the <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genetic map with the wheat 2D consensus genetic map was unambiguous, with the exception of some single crossovers that were probably due to repetitive elements (dotted lines in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). The genome sequence also provided the basis for the identification of more than 860,126 simple sequence repeats (SSRs), with trimers (37.7%) and tetramers (27.5%) as the most abundant SSR types (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>). Together with the 711,907 SNPs identified by resequencing a roughly fivefold coverage of a second accession, Y2280 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a>), the genomic resources reported here will promote map-based gene cloning and marker-assisted selection in wheat.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="&#xA;                        An integrated genetic map of &#xA;                        Ae.âtauschii&#xA;                         chromosome 2D.&#xA;                      "><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: 
                        <b>An integrated genetic map of </b>
                        <b><i>Ae.âtauschii</i></b>
                        <b> chromosome 2D.</b>
                      </b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12028/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12028/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12028_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12028/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12028_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="924" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genetic map was integrated with markers, scaffolds and mapped QTLs to assist in marker development and map-based cloning. Left: the <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> molecular map used for synteny alignment in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> was aligned to chromosomeâ2D (November 2011 consensus map, CMap; <a href="http://ccg.murdoch.edu.au/cmap/ccg-live">http://ccg.murdoch.edu.au/cmap/ccg-live</a>) where sequence information was available. The original marker at a location is retained in CMap as a synonym. Right: within CMap, details for QTL locations are provided at a greater magnification to show all the markers in the regions of interest. The dotted lines indicate an ambiguous relationship that is most probably due to repetitive sequences.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM75">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12028/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>With its high base accuracy and nearly complete set of gene sequences, the <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> draft genome sequence provides an essential reference for studying Dâgenome diversity by re-sequencing additional accessions. Over the past half century, the introduction of new Dâgenome diversity into synthetic wheat has been a major effort to expand bread wheat genetic diversity and to create environmentally resilient lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Talbert, L. E., Smith, L. Y. &amp; Blake, N. K. More than one origin of hexaploid wheat is indicated by sequence comparison of low-copy DNA. Genome 41, 402â407 (1998)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d56593e2006">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Trethowan, R. M. &amp; Mujeeb-Kazi, A. Novel germplasm resources for improving environmental stress tolerance of hexaploid wheat. Crop Sci. 48, 1255â1265 (2008)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d56593e2009">27</a></sup>. The <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> genome sequence should aid in identifying new elite alleles for agriculturally important traits to alleviate the worsening plight of global climate and environment changes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Trethowan, R. M. &amp; Mujeeb-Kazi, A. Novel germplasm resources for improving environmental stress tolerance of hexaploid wheat. Crop Sci. 48, 1255â1265 (2008)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d56593e2016">27</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>We selected <i>Ae.âtauschii</i> (2<i>n</i> = 14) accession AL8/78 for sequencing. Plants were grown at 25âÂ°C in a darkened chamber for two weeks; DNA was extracted from leaf tissue and purified with a standard phenol/chloroform extraction protocol. Sequencing libraries were constructed and sequenced on Illumina next-generation sequencing platforms (GAII and HiSequation (2000)). High-quality reads were assembled with SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d56593e2034">3</a></sup>. Repeat sequences were identified by combining <i>de novo</i> approaches and sequence similarity at the nucleotide and protein levels. Gene models were predicted by combining homology-based, <i>de novo</i> and RNA-Seq-based methods. RNA-Seq reads were assembled with CAP3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Huang, X. &amp; Madan, A. CAP3: a DNA sequence assembly program. Genome Res. 9, 868â877 (1999)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d56593e2044">28</a>) and CD-Hit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Li, W. &amp; Godzik, A. Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 22, 1658â1659 (2006)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d56593e2048">29</a></sup> and were mapped to the draft genome with Tophat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105â1111 (2009)" href="/articles/nature12028#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d56593e2052">30</a></sup>. See <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12028#MOESM76">Supplementary Information</a> for details and additional analyses.</p></div></div></section>

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Data deposits</h3>
                <p>The genome sequence and the annotation are available from the National Centre for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) as BioProject ID PRJNA182898. This Whole Genome Shotgun project is deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AOCO000000000">AOCO000000000</a>. The version described in this paper is the first version, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AOCO010000000">AOCO010000000</a>. The Illumina sequencing reads are available in the Sequence Read Archive under accession number <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA030526">SRA030526</a>, RNA-Seq sequences under <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA062662">SRA062662</a>, and resequencing short reads under <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA063175">SRA063175</a>. Genomic data are also available at the Comprehensive Library for Modern Biotechnology (CLiMB) repository under <a href="https://doi.org/10.5524/100054">doi:10.5524/100054</a>.</p>

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">An A to D of wheat genomes</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>The hexaploid genome of bread wheat <i>Triticum aestivum</i>, designated AABBDD, evolved as a result of hybridization between three ancestral grasses. Two papers published in the issue of <i>Nature</i> present genome sequences and analysis of two of these wheat progenitors. First, the genome sequence of the diploid wild wheat <i>T. urartu</i> (ancestor of the A genome), which resembles cultivated wheat more strongly than either <i>Aegilops speltoides</i> (the B ancestor) or <i>Ae. tauschii</i> (the D donor). And second, the <i>Ae. tauschii</i> genome, together with an analysis of its transcriptome. These genomes and their analyses will be powerful tools for the study of complex, polyploid wheat genomes and a valuable resource for genetic improvement of wheat.</p></div></section></div></aside>
